+"""Lane B_gen — beat-both run-5 generation faithfulness + completeness fixes.
+
+Covers three bugs from outputs/audits/beatboth5/FULL_BUG_LIST.md, all OFFLINE and
+deterministic (no network, no spend — judge + LLM + retrieval are monkeypatched):
+
+  BB5-F02 (#1176, faithfulness) — clinical overgeneralization: a sentence that DROPS a
+    scope-narrowing qualifier (strain/subgroup/timepoint) from its cited span is NOT entailed
+    by that span, so the BINDING entailment gate must DROP it under enforce mode. The drb_76
+    example: span "S. cerevisiae var. boulardii probiotics are not recommended ..." widened to
+    "current evidence advises against routine probiotic use ..." (strain dropped). These tests
+    exercise the REAL benchmark binding gate (generator/provenance_generator.strict_verify ->
+    verify_sentence_provenance, the verifier scripts/run_honest_sweep_r3.py actually uses) with a
+    FakeJudge returning NEUTRAL. They prove the GATE MECHANICS: a NEUTRAL verdict on a widened
+    sentence is dropped fail-closed (incl. the local-window re-judge). They do NOT prove the real
+    Gemma model returns NEUTRAL for boulardii->probiotics — empirically it returned ENTAILED
+    (drb_76 manifest: the poster sentence is in entailed_count, and the rendered report kept it).
+    Catching the real case is an out-of-scope prompt lever (entailment_judge._ENTAILMENT_PROMPT,
+    whose NEUTRAL examples are narrowing-only) — reported, not fixed in this lane.
+
+  BB5-C06 (#1178, completeness) — run_gate_b forced PG_FRAME_PREFER_ABSTRACT=1 with
+    frame_fetcher's _FULLTEXT_ENTITY_TYPES being trial/review-only, so EVERY narrative /
+    source-critical entity (economic/policy/mechanism/cohort/regulatory/legal) skipped its OA
+    full text and read only the ~500-char abstract. Fix broadens the keep-full-text set via
+    PG_FRAME_FULLTEXT_ENTITY_TYPES (set before the lazy frame_fetcher import freezes it).
+
+  BB5-C07 (#1178, completeness) — a legacy (non-V30) section that yields ZERO verified sentences
+    was marked dropped_due_to_failure=True with empty verified_text and SILENTLY VANISHED at
+    render (drb_75 "Safety" disappeared on a clinical-safety question). Fix renders an explicit
+    gap-disclosure stub (mirroring the V30 slot path) tagged is_gap_stub=True so the section ships
+    with a trace and downstream verified-prose consumers can still skip it.
+"""
+
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import asyncio
+
+import pytest
+
+from src.polaris_graph.clinical_generator import strict_verify as _judge_home
+from src.polaris_graph.generator import multi_section_generator as _msg
+from src.polaris_graph.generator.provenance_generator import (
+    verify_sentence_provenance,
+)
+
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Shared FakeJudge — mirrors tests/polaris_graph/test_provenance_generator_entailment.py
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+class _FakeJudge:
+    """Returns a fixed verdict on EVERY judge call (so the binding gate's narrow-span
+    judge AND its local-window re-judge both see the same verdict)."""
+
+    def __init__(self, verdict: str, reason: str = "fake") -> None:
+        self.verdict = verdict
+        self.reason = reason
+        self.calls: list[tuple[str, str]] = []
+
+    def judge(self, sentence: str, span: str) -> tuple[str, str]:
+        self.calls.append((sentence, span))
+        return self.verdict, self.reason
+
+
+def _install_judge(monkeypatch, fake: _FakeJudge) -> None:
+    """Replace the judge singleton + factory on the judge's canonical home module so
+    verify_sentence_provenance (which lazy-imports _get_judge from there) picks up the fake."""
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_judge_home, "_JUDGE_SINGLETON", fake, raising=False)
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_judge_home, "_get_judge", lambda: fake)
+
+
+@pytest.fixture(autouse=True)
+def _reset_judge_telemetry():
+    _judge_home.reset_judge_telemetry()
+    yield
+
+
+def _pool(direct_quote: str, evidence_id: str = "ev_strain") -> dict:
+    return {
+        evidence_id: {
+            "evidence_id": evidence_id,
+            "direct_quote": direct_quote,
+            "url": "https://example.org/finnish-registry",
+            "tier": "T1",
+        },
+    }
+
+
+# ===========================================================================
+# BB5-F02 — strain-generalization (clinical overgeneralization) drop regression
+# ===========================================================================
+
+# The cited SPAN names the SPECIFIC strain (S. cerevisiae var. boulardii). Verbatim from the
+# drb_76 source (outputs/audits/beatboth5/drb_76_polaris.md:44).
+_BOULARDII_SPAN = (
+    "The authors conclude that S. cerevisiae var. boulardii probiotics are not "
+    "recommended for patients with indwelling catheters, who are immunocompromised, "
+    "or who are critically ill."
+)
+
+# The SHIPPED sentence DROPS the strain qualifier and widens to a blanket "probiotic" caution.
+# Verbatim widened claim from the same report. It shares >=2 content words with the span
+# (probiotic / patients / immunocompromised|immunosuppression / catheters|catheter / critical),
+# carries no decimals, so the mechanical checks pass and the sentence REACHES the entailment judge.
+def _widened_sentence(evidence_id: str = "ev_strain") -> str:
+    span_len = len(_BOULARDII_SPAN)
+    return (
+        "Current evidence advises against routine probiotic use in patients with "
+        "central venous catheters, immunosuppression, or critical illness "
+        f"[#ev:{evidence_id}:0-{span_len}]."
+    )
+
+
+def test_f02_strain_widening_dropped_under_enforce(monkeypatch):
+    """The binding benchmark gate MUST drop the strain-widened sentence on a NEUTRAL verdict.
+
+    Proves gate mechanics: a sentence the cited span does NOT entail (dropped strain qualifier)
+    is dropped fail-closed in enforce mode. Does NOT prove the real model returns NEUTRAL here.
+    """
+    monkeypatch.setenv("PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT", "enforce")
+    fake = _FakeJudge("NEUTRAL", "span specifies S. boulardii; sentence generalizes to 'probiotics'")
+    _install_judge(monkeypatch, fake)
+
+    pool = _pool(_BOULARDII_SPAN)
+    result = verify_sentence_provenance(_widened_sentence(), pool)
+
+    assert result.is_verified is False, (
+        "a strain-widened (qualifier-dropped) sentence judged NEUTRAL must be dropped"
+    )
+    assert any(
+        r.startswith("entailment_failed:") for r in result.failure_reasons
+    ), f"expected entailment_failed drop reason, got {result.failure_reasons}"
+    # The judge must actually have been reached (mechanical checks did not pre-empt it).
+    assert fake.calls, "entailment judge must run (mechanical checks must not pre-empt it)"
+
+
+def test_f02_faithful_paraphrase_kept_when_entailed(monkeypatch):
+    """Positive control: a strain-PRESERVING paraphrase the span entails must be KEPT —
+    the gate strengthening must not false-drop faithful clinical prose."""
+    monkeypatch.setenv("PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT", "enforce")
+    fake = _FakeJudge("ENTAILED")
+    _install_judge(monkeypatch, fake)
+
+    pool = _pool(_BOULARDII_SPAN)
+    span_len = len(_BOULARDII_SPAN)
+    faithful = (
+        "S. cerevisiae var. boulardii probiotics are not recommended for "
+        "immunocompromised patients or those with indwelling catheters "
+        f"[#ev:ev_strain:0-{span_len}]."
+    )
+    result = verify_sentence_provenance(faithful, pool)
+    assert result.is_verified is True, (
+        f"strain-preserving faithful paraphrase must pass, got {result.failure_reasons}"
+    )
+
+
+def test_f02_off_mode_does_not_drop_widening(monkeypatch):
+    """Honest scope boundary: with the gate OFF the widened sentence is NOT dropped and the
+    judge never runs — confirms the drop is the ENFORCE gate's doing, not a mechanical check."""
+    monkeypatch.setenv("PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT", "off")
+    fake = _FakeJudge("NEUTRAL")
+    _install_judge(monkeypatch, fake)
+
+    pool = _pool(_BOULARDII_SPAN)
+    result = verify_sentence_provenance(_widened_sentence(), pool)
+    assert result.is_verified is True, "off mode must keep the sentence (no entailment gate)"
+    assert fake.calls == [], "off mode must not invoke the judge"
+
+
+# ===========================================================================

+def test_c06_run_gate_b_sets_fulltext_env_before_prefer_abstract():

+def test_c06_broadened_types_keep_fulltext_path_in_frame_fetcher(monkeypatch):
+    """End-to-end env wiring: with the broadened set applied, frame_fetcher's entity-type gate
+    must KEEP the full-text path (NOT skip:prefer_abstract) for a mechanism/cohort/economic entity.
+
+    Exercises the real frame_fetcher module constants under the broadened env — forcing a reimport
+    so the import-time freeze reads the broadened value."""
+    import importlib
+
+    from scripts.dr_benchmark.run_gate_b import _BENCHMARK_FULLTEXT_ENTITY_TYPES
+
+    monkeypatch.setenv("PG_FRAME_PREFER_ABSTRACT", "1")
+    monkeypatch.setenv("PG_FRAME_FULLTEXT_ENTITY_TYPES", _BENCHMARK_FULLTEXT_ENTITY_TYPES)
+
+    import src.polaris_graph.retrieval.frame_fetcher as ff
+    ff = importlib.reload(ff)
+    try:
+        fulltext = ff._FULLTEXT_ENTITY_TYPES
+        # Narrative entities are now IN the keep-full-text set -> entity_prefers_abstract is False.
+        for narrative in ("mechanism_primary", "cohort_primary", "economic_report", "court_decision"):
+            assert narrative in fulltext, (
+                f"{narrative!r} not in reloaded _FULLTEXT_ENTITY_TYPES under broadened env"
+            )
+        # And prefer-abstract is still ON (we only stop the SKIP for full-text-needing entities).
+        assert ff._FRAME_PREFER_ABSTRACT is True
+    finally:
+        # Restore module to the ambient (off) env so we don't pollute later tests.
+        monkeypatch.undo()
+        importlib.reload(ff)
+

+# ===========================================================================
+# BB5-C07 — dropped section renders an explicit gap stub instead of vanishing
+# ===========================================================================

+def test_c07_gap_stub_sentence_is_marker_less():
+    """The gap-disclosure stub must carry NO provenance/citation marker — fabricating a citation
+    for a non-claim would be a faithfulness defect (it is a disclosure, not a verified claim)."""
+    stub = _msg._GAP_STUB_SENTENCE
+    assert "[#ev:" not in stub and "[#calc:" not in stub, "stub must not carry a provenance token"
+    import re
+    assert not re.search(r"\[\d+\]", stub), "stub must not carry a numbered citation marker"
+    assert "curator-actionable gap" in stub
+    assert "did not" not in stub.lower() or "survive" in stub.lower()  # honest disclosure wording
+
+
+def test_c07_section_result_is_gap_stub_defaults_false():
+    """Byte-identical default: a normal SectionResult is not a gap stub."""
+    sr = _msg.SectionResult(
+        title="Efficacy", focus="", ev_ids_assigned=[], raw_draft="", rewritten_draft="",
+        verified_text="A verified claim [1].", biblio_slice=[],
+        sentences_verified=1, sentences_dropped=0, regen_attempted=False,
+        dropped_due_to_failure=False,
+    )
+    assert sr.is_gap_stub is False
+
+
+def test_c07_gap_stub_section_survives_render_and_assembly_skip():
+    """A gap-stub section must NOT be skipped by the render/assembly predicate
+    (`dropped_due_to_failure or not verified_text`) — that is exactly what made it vanish — while
+    a verified-prose consumer can still skip it via is_gap_stub / sentences_verified==0."""
+    stub_sr = _msg.SectionResult(
+        title="Safety", focus="", ev_ids_assigned=[], raw_draft="", rewritten_draft="",
+        verified_text=_msg._GAP_STUB_SENTENCE, biblio_slice=[],
+        sentences_verified=0, sentences_dropped=5, regen_attempted=True,
+        dropped_due_to_failure=False, is_gap_stub=True,
+    )
+    # Render/assembly skip predicate used at run_honest_sweep_r3.py:5232 + assembly:5363.
+    render_skipped = stub_sr.dropped_due_to_failure or not stub_sr.verified_text
+    assert render_skipped is False, "gap-stub section must render (not vanish)"
+    # Verified-prose consumer (Key Findings / BB5-P07) skip signals.
+    assert stub_sr.is_gap_stub is True
+    assert stub_sr.sentences_verified == 0
+
+
+def test_c07_run_section_renders_stub_when_zero_verified(monkeypatch):
+    """Exercise the REAL _run_section line-2287 logic: when strict_verify keeps ZERO sentences,
+    the returned SectionResult must (a) NOT be dropped_due_to_failure, (b) carry the gap stub as
+    verified_text, (c) be tagged is_gap_stub=True, (d) report sentences_verified == 0.
+
+    Fully offline: _call_section (LLM), strict_verify, the rewrite, the repair loop, the M-41c
+    filter, and the citation resolver are all monkeypatched to force the zero-verified path."""
+
+    class _ZeroReport:
+        def __init__(self) -> None:
+            self.kept_sentences: list = []
+            self.dropped_sentences: list = ["s1", "s2", "s3"]
+            self.total_kept = 0
+            self.total_dropped = 3
+            self.total_in = 3
+
+    async def _fake_call_section(*args, **kwargs):
+        return ("raw draft prose", 10, 20, {})
+
+    def _fake_strict_verify(rewritten, evidence_pool):
+        return _ZeroReport()
+
+    def _fake_rewrite(raw, evidence_pool):
+        return (raw, [], [])
+
+    def _fake_m41c(kept):
+        # No kept sentences -> nothing to filter; no policy drops.
+        return (list(kept), [])
+
+    def _fake_resolve(kept_sentences, evidence_pool):
+        # Zero kept -> empty resolved text + empty bibliography. The stub OVERRIDES this.
+        return ("", [])
+
+    def _fake_normalize(text):
+        return text
+
+    async def _fake_repair(*args, **kwargs):
+        # Repair recovers nothing (keep zero). Telemetry object with attempts==0 short-circuits
+        # the logging branch in _run_section.
+        class _Tel:
+            attempts = 0
+            successes = 0
+            recovery_rate = 0.0
+            null_drops = 0
+            token_set_violations = 0
+            re_verify_failures = 0
+            api_failures = 0
+            input_tokens = 0
+            output_tokens = 0
+        return ([], ["s1", "s2", "s3"], _Tel())
+
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_msg, "_call_section", _fake_call_section)
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_msg, "strict_verify", _fake_strict_verify)
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_msg, "_rewrite_draft_with_spans", _fake_rewrite)
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_msg, "filter_underframed_trial_sentences", _fake_m41c)
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_msg, "resolve_provenance_to_citations", _fake_resolve)
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_msg, "_normalize_citation_punctuation", _fake_normalize)
+    # The repair loop lazy-imports from this module; patch the source symbol.
+    import src.polaris_graph.generator.sentence_repair as _sr_mod
+    monkeypatch.setattr(_sr_mod, "repair_dropped_section_sentences", _fake_repair)
+
+    section = _msg.SectionPlan(title="Safety", focus="adverse events", ev_ids=["ev_a"])
+    pool = {"ev_a": {"evidence_id": "ev_a", "direct_quote": "Some safety text.", "tier": "T1"}}
+
+    result = asyncio.run(
+        _msg._run_section(
+            section, pool,
+            model="x", temperature=0.2, max_tokens_per_section=500,
+            min_kept_fraction=0.4,
+        )
+    )
+
+    assert result.is_gap_stub is True, "zero-verified section must be tagged a gap stub"
+    assert result.dropped_due_to_failure is False, "gap stub must NOT be dropped (would vanish)"
+    assert result.verified_text == _msg._GAP_STUB_SENTENCE, "gap stub text must be rendered"
+    assert result.sentences_verified == 0, "gap stub carries zero verified sentences"
+    assert result.title == "Safety", "the planned section title is preserved"
+
+
+def test_c07_no_evidence_section_renders_stub_not_vanish():
+    """Sibling vanish path: a planned section with NO assigned evidence in the pool must render
+    the no-evidence gap stub (dropped_due_to_failure=False, is_gap_stub=True) instead of silently
+    disappearing. Hits the early-return before any LLM/strict_verify, so it is trivially offline."""
+    section = _msg.SectionPlan(title="Safety", focus="adverse events", ev_ids=["ev_missing"])
+    # The pool does NOT contain ev_missing -> ev_subset is empty -> early-return path.
+    pool = {"ev_other": {"evidence_id": "ev_other", "direct_quote": "Unrelated text.", "tier": "T1"}}
+
+    result = asyncio.run(
+        _msg._run_section(
+            section, pool,
+            model="x", temperature=0.2, max_tokens_per_section=500,
+            min_kept_fraction=0.4,
+        )
+    )
+    assert result.is_gap_stub is True
+    assert result.dropped_due_to_failure is False, "no-evidence section must NOT vanish"
+    assert result.verified_text == _msg._NO_EVIDENCE_GAP_STUB_SENTENCE
+    assert result.sentences_verified == 0
+    assert result.error == "no_evidence_in_pool", "cause preserved for telemetry"
+    assert result.title == "Safety"
+

Fern\u00e1ndez d , and Christian Rammer c > a Dept. of Management, Strategy and Innovation, KU Leuven, Belgium > b Centre for R&D Monitoring (ECOOM) at KU Leuven, Belgium > c Department Economics of Innovation and Industrial Dynamics, ZEW - Leibniz Centre for European Economic Research, Germany > d Dept. of Economics, KU Leuven, Belgium This version: March 2023 Abstract Artificial Intelligence (AI) is often regarded as the next general-purpose technology with a rapid, penetrating, and far-reaching use over a broad number of industrial sectors. The main feature of new general-purpose technology is to enable new ways of production that may increase productivity. However, to date, only a few studies have investigated the likely productivity effects of AI at the firm-level, presumably due to limited data availability. We exploit unique survey data on firms\u2019 adoption of AI technology and estimate its productivity effects with a sample of German firms. We employ both a cross-sectional dataset and a panel database. To address the potential endogeneity of AI adoption, we also implement IV estimators. We find positive and significant associ\n\n[...]\n\nnd model variables while Section 5 contains the results of our empirical analysis, including several robustness checks. Finally, Section 6 discusses the findings and identifies further research questions. # 2 Artificial Intelligence and Productivity 2.1 AI as a productivity-enhancing technology or faltering innovation? There are several potential channels by which AI can propel firm productivity. For example, machine learning advances have encouraged cheaper and better predictive analyses allowing \n\n[...]\n\nms, empirical research has so far mainly focused on three areas or approaches: (i) industrial robots and automation of tasks; (ii) patents or scientific publications related to AI; and (iii) data-driven managerial decisions or the use of big data. 6 2.2 AI, Robots, and productivity Early empirical work on likely productivity effects of AI used data on robots since automation through robots is increasingly using AI technology. However, not all robotization rests on AI technology, and AI-based robots\n\n[...]\n\nl nature of robots, which makes them a tangible capital asset easy to measure and track, on top of the availability of public data (e.g., IRF data on industrial robots), has retained the attention on this area of most of the existing empirical work. 2.3 AI-related patents and productivity Another stream of literature attempts to identify the impact of AI technologies through patent data. In general, patents are a relevant driver for the productivity growth and performance of 8 firms. For example, V\n\n[...]\n\nowth in annual sales in AI inventors with at least one granted patent \u2013 especially SMEs \u2013 compared to firms with only non-granted AI patent applications. Another recent study investigates the impact of patents associated with the so-called \u201cIndustry 4.0\u201d technologies, which would include AI methods, on the economic performance of firms. Behrens and Trunschke (2020) employed a panel dataset of German firms and found that the marginal effect of an additional \u201c4.0 patent\u201d would increase firms' sales by 8.3%, which diminishes by firm size (see as well Venturini (2022)). Further studies analyzing the contribution of AI adoption on firm performance using patent information are Yang (2022), De Prato et al. (2018), Cockburn et al. (2019), Alderucci et al. (2020), and Damioli et al. (2021). 2.4 AI-related jobs and productivity Another way of measuring firm investment in AI is through analyzing jobs that require AI skills. Acemoglu et al. (2020) used job posting data to assign a measure of AI exposure based on firms\u2019 occupation structure an\n\n[...]\n\ninnish firms. They found that AI adoption increases productivity, but only for large firms. Early adopters do not experience productivity increases. There is evidence of at least three years delay between the adoption of AI and productivity effects. 2.5 AI, Big Data in the business, and productivity A further group of papers studies the impact of data-driven decisions or the use of big data on firm performance. AI-related methods such as machine learning algorithms and deep neural networks are usua\n\n[...]\n\nr related works). Notwithstanding the close relationship between big data and AI, not all big data analyses involve the development and adoption of AI, nor do the different varieties of AI applications in firms necessarily involve big data analysis. 2.6 AI measured through firm surveys In recent years, firm surveys have been used to collect data on the adoption of AI technologies by firms. While these surveys are useful to inform about the diffusion of AI technologies (see 10 Montagnier et al. 2020\n\n[...]\n\nmy variable, we will also use an AI index that measures the intensity of AI use in the firm. b. Different from other national innovation surveys, the German survey is designed a\n\n[...]\n\nh a standardized questionnaire that can be answered both on paper and online. The response rate of the MIP is between 25 and 35%. As shown in Table 1, our cross-sectional sample contains 5,851 firms out of which 409 can be classified as AI users (about 7%). Among the AI-using firms, the average value of our AI intensity variable amounts to 12.9%, i.e., the average firm used 2.5 out of the 20 possible combinations of AI methods and areas of application. About 60% of AI-using firms used AI in products or services, 56% for the automation of processes, 34% for data analysis, and 22% for the interaction with clients (see Rammer et al. 2022). 4.3 Other variables in the production function The production function is estimated for the financial year 2018. Journal of Labor Economics, 42(2), 591-634. https://doi.org/10.1086/723205 Robots and Employment: Evidence from Japan, 1978-2017\u2217 Daisuke Adachi\u2020 Daiji Kawaguchi\u2021 Yukiko U. Saito\u00a7 October 6, 2022 Abstract This paper studies the relationship between industrial robots and employment in Japan based on a unique dataset \n\n[...]\n\nnstructing a Bartik-style industry-year-level robot price index to estimate the impact of robot price on robot adoption and employment. In our empirical analysis, we show that a one percent decrease in the price of robots increased robot adoption by 1.54 percent. Perhaps more surprisingly, we also \ufb01nd that a one percent decrease in the robot price increased employment by 0.44 percent, so a large availability of robots actually raised employment, suggesting that the scale e\ufb00ect induced by robot adoption was substantial and dominated the substitution e\ufb00ect. As we found a large and signi\ufb01cant elasticity of industrial real o\n\n[...]\n\necond we regressed the changes in labor market outcomes on the number of robots using the instrumental variable of the shift-share measure of robot prices across CZs. Our results indicate that one robot unit per 1,000 workers increases employment by 2.2 percent, corroborating our industry-level \ufb01nding that a decline in robot prices increases employment. This contrasts with the \ufb01nding by AR, whose corresponding estimate was -1.6 percent. We also \ufb01nd that the across- industry-within-region spillovers are limited, as the positive employment e\ufb00ect was predominantly found in the manufacturing sector but not in the non-manufacturing sector or for those who were not employed and \n\n[...]\n\n the change in labor demand is characterized by two e\ufb00ects, (i) a shift in the task allocation from labor to robots and (ii) a cost savings due to EAR. Finally, we use the change in the robot price as a proxy for EAR to derive a regression equation. 2.1 Setup We consider an economy with T periods indexed by t = 0, 1, . . .,T and I industries indexed by i = 1, . . ., I. Production factors are labor and robots, where robots are de\ufb01ned as the combination of robots used in each application, as describe\n\n[...]\n\nat even under this assumption, the price of robots remains relevant for factor demand. Speci\ufb01cally, as we see in the following sections, labor demand is a\ufb00ected by both the task-based force (automation) and the standard force (factor price changes). 2.2 Solution Solving this model involves the characterization of the following producer task allocation problem. Let us focus on industry i in year t. Since tasks are ordered according to robots\u2019 comparative advantage, the optimal task allocation can be\n\n[...]\n\n task by robots or workers. Under the assumption in equation (5), a cost-minimizing producer chooses the threshold \u03c9\u2217 it = \u00af\u03c9it so that tasks \u03c9 0 as well as an expansion of the robot tasks d \u00af\u03c9it > 0, or cost savings \u2212Si0d \u00af\u03c9it > 0, resulting in \u03c1 > 0.6 Note that \u03c1 captures the correlation in a reduced form without any structural interpretation. Combining equations (8), (9), (10) and (11), we can derive the following equation: d ln Lit = aL t + bLd ln pR it + eit, (12) 6This condition holds if the \n\n[...]\n\nequation (12), the required variables are the robot price, robot quantity, and employment by industry i in year t. We begin with a description of our unique robot data and its summary statistics, followed by data on employment and control variables. 3.1 Robot Data Our main dataset is derived from a Japan Robot Association (JARA) establishment-level survey of Japanese robot producers, the same Japanese data source as that used in the International Federation of Robotics (IFR) dataset, a leading info\n\n[...]\n\nn to capital, as reported by Miyagawa et al. (2018). 13 con\ufb01rming the quality of the JARA data. We also describe a major classi\ufb01cation di\ufb00erence between JARA and IFR, clean-room robots, which we will check does not a\ufb00ect our empirical results later. 3.2 Industrial Adoption, Application, and Price of Robots In this section, we use the robot data described in Section 3.1 to provide descriptive statistics which not only increase our understanding of Japanese robot adoption but also lead to the idea and rationale for our identi\ufb01cation strategy. After describing the industry shares of robots, we show some relevant tren\n\n[...]\n\nsition of the sales of robots to destination industries in the \u201cTotal\u201d column,10 and we see that a substantial share of robot purchases are by the electric machine and transportation machine (including automobile) industries which together comprised 68.2 percent of all domestic absorption in 2017. Given this feature of the data, we focus on the application shares at the industry level, with an emphasis on these two industries. At the same time, this leads to a caution about our identi\ufb01cation strategy in that these buyers are large, so their demand might a\ufb00ect observed unit values. We address this issue in depth in Section 4.3. src\polaris_graph\generator\multi_section_generator.py:459:- **M-40: Mechanism section is the narrative-depth lever.** When AT LEAST 3 evidence rows in the summary above contain mechanism-of-action vocabulary — in either the `title:` field or the statement body — you MUST include "Mechanism" as one of the outline sections (5 by default, 6 when regulatory evidence is also present per M-41a above). Trigger vocabulary (any of, case-insensitive): "mechanism", "pharmacokinetic", "pharmacodynamic", "receptor", "half-life", "bioavailability", "metabolism", "agonist", "antagonist", "binding", "signaling", "pathway", "kinetic". A research-grade synthesis explains WHY the intervention works, not only WHETHER it works. Top-tier Deep Research outputs (GPT-5.4 DR, Gemini 3.1 Pro DR) dedicate a full section to mechanism/pharmacology for any clinical efficacy question; a report without it reads as a short brief rather than a deep synthesis. This rule is generalizable: in materials/chemistry a Mechanism section covers reaction pathway / phase transition / interface chemistry; in policy it covers causal pathway / incentive mechanism / enforcement mechanism; in finance it covers transmission channel / market microstructure.

src\polaris_graph\generator\multi_section_generator.py:1193:12. **Primary-study framing (M-32, for claim-frame rigor)**: When you name a primary study, trial, cohort, experiment, or any individually identifiable empirical data source, and the cited evidence rows contain the structured metadata, provide the study's FULL FRAME in the FIRST sentence that introduces it: (a) sample size or cohort size (e.g. N=1879), (b) baseline value of the outcome being discussed (e.g. mean baseline [PRIMARY_METRIC]=[VALUE], baseline [SECONDARY_METRIC]=[VALUE]), (c) comparator / control / background condition (e.g. versus [COMPARATOR], versus placebo on [BACKGROUND], versus standard [REFERENCE_CONDITION]), and (d) the primary endpoint + timepoint (e.g. [ENDPOINT] change at [TIMEPOINT], [N-YEAR] [OUTCOME_TYPE], cycle-life to [THRESHOLD] retention). If the evidence row carries this structured metadata, you MUST emit it in that first sentence — do not compress N/baseline/endpoint into a single percent-reduction when the evidence carries the full frame. This is what distinguishes a research-grade deep synthesis from a news-style summary. Example template: "In [STUDY NAME], [STUDY_DESIGN_SUMMARY] randomized N=[SAMPLE_SIZE] participants with baseline [OUTCOME]=[BASELINE_VALUE] to [INTERVENTION] versus [COMPARATOR]; [PRIMARY_ENDPOINT] at [TIMEPOINT] was [RESULT] [ev_X]." Subsequent sentences about the same study may reference it by short name without re-framing. Generalizable beyond clinical: a materials paper gets composition + baseline performance + test condition + measured outcome; a cohort study gets population + baseline metric + intervention + outcome; a financial filing gets period + baseline metric + policy/benchmark + reported outcome.
src\polaris_graph\generator\multi_section_generator.py:1194:12b. **Claim-frame hard constraint (M-38, eliminating under-framed study mentions)**: Rule #12 is asymmetric and STRICT — when you name a specific study, trial, cohort, or experiment by its short name (phase-N trial identifier in clinical; long-run battery cycling test in materials; named longitudinal cohort in epidemiology; named regulatory docket in policy), that sentence — or the IMMEDIATELY PRECEDING sentence in the same paragraph — MUST carry at LEAST THREE frame elements drawn from: sample size / cohort N; baseline value; comparator / control arm; specific dose or intervention level; primary endpoint; timepoint; effect size WITH uncertainty (CI, SD, or p-value). If you cannot produce three of those elements from the cited evidence, DO NOT name the study by its short name — phrase the sentence generically as "one randomized trial showed ... [ev_X]" or "a prospective cohort in the target population reported ... [ev_X]" or "one pooled analysis found ... [ev_X]" or "a long-run cycling test reported ... [ev_X]" instead. This hard floor prevents the failure mode where a sentence names a specific study but gives only a single effect-size number without N, baseline, or comparator — producing a news-style summary mis-labelled with a primary-study name. Concrete templates (use placeholders): GOOD: "In [STUDY NAME] (N=[SAMPLE_SIZE], baseline [PRIMARY_METRIC]=[BASELINE_VALUE]), [INTERVENTION_ARM] reduced [ENDPOINT] by [EFFECT_SIZE] versus [COMPARATOR_ARM] at [TIMEPOINT] [ev_X]." GOOD (generic when frame is unavailable): "A pre-planned pooled analysis of two phase-3 trials reported [ENDPOINT]=[VALUE] at [TIMEPOINT] [ev_X]." — no short-name attribution because pooled data lack per-trial N. BAD (under-framed, must be rewritten): "[STUDY NAME] showed that [INTERVENTION] reduced [ENDPOINT] more than [COMPARATOR] [ev_X]." — names the study with only one frame element (effect direction); rewrite as "A head-to-head trial of [INTERVENTION] versus [COMPARATOR] reported greater [ENDPOINT] reduction with [INTERVENTION] [ev_X]" which drops the study name because the frame is too thin. BAD (under-framed, must be rewritten): "[STUDY NAME] found median time to [THRESHOLD] was [TIMEPOINT] [ev_X]." — names study with only endpoint + effect; rewrite as "One pooled analysis of two phase-3 trials found median time to [THRESHOLD] was [TIMEPOINT] [ev_X]" ONLY if the cited evidence confirms pooled data across two trials. This rule is what converts a LOSE_BOTH on Claim frames into a competitive synthesis.

 succeeded in 14666ms:
    1: """Strict verifier per CLAUDE.md §9.1 invariant 3.
    2: 
    3: Per-sentence check enforcing:
    4:   (a) at least one provenance token present
    5:   (b) every token references an evidence_id in the pool
    6:   (c) span bounds are valid (0 <= start <= end <= len(text))
    7:   (d) every decimal in the sentence appears in the cited spans
    8:   (e) sentence and span share >= MIN_CONTENT_OVERLAP content words
    9:   (f) I-bug-092: span semantically ENTAILS the sentence's specific claims
   10:       (gated behind PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT, defaults off)
   11: 
   12: Checks (a)-(e) are mechanical "provenance hygiene" — they bound a claim to
   13: the same TOPIC as its span. Check (f) is "provenance correctness" — it
   14: verifies the span actually entails the sentence's specific facts. The
   15: audit on 2026-05-09 found that a capable generator can pass (a)-(e) by
   16: producing topically-overlapping prose that introduces unsourced specifics
   17: (e.g. inserting "pancreatic β-cells", "lipid metabolism", "energy storage"
   18: into a span that says only "synergistic actions on insulin secretion,
   19: glucagon suppression, appetite regulation, and adipocyte metabolism").
   20: 
   21: Returns a (verifier_pass: bool, drop_reason: str | None) pair, or directly
   22: constructs a VerifiedSentence for use by the generator orchestrator.
   23: 
   24: Tunables (read at call time so tests can override):
   25:   PG_PROVENANCE_MIN_CONTENT_OVERLAP — minimum shared content words (default 2)
   26:   PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT       — "off" | "warn" | "enforce" (default)
   27:                                        off:     skip check (f), pre-I-bug-092 behavior
   28:                                                 — operator override; production runs
   29:                                                   ship without provenance-correctness
   30:                                                   enforcement
   31:                                        warn:    run check (f), log violations,
   32:                                                 do NOT drop (collect telemetry)
   33:                                        enforce: run check (f), drop on
   34:                                                 NEUTRAL or CONTRADICTED verdict
   35:                                                 (default per I-bug-095, validated
   36:                                                 empirically by I-bug-094 live test)
   37:   PG_ENTAILMENT_MODEL               — entailment judge model
   38:                                        (default: google/gemma-4-31b-it,
   39:                                         the two-family evaluator)
   40: """
   41: 
   42: from __future__ import annotations
   43: 
   44: import json
   45: import logging

   47: import re

   61: 
   62: logger = logging.getLogger(__name__)
   63: 
   64: 
   65: # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
   66: # Tunables
   67: # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
   68: 
   69: DEFAULT_MIN_CONTENT_OVERLAP = 2
   70: 
   71: # Minimal English stopword list. Strict-verify's content-word overlap should
   72: # count substantive vocabulary, not function words. This is intentionally
   73: # small — the rule is conservative ("at least 2 shared content words")
   74: # so even a short stoplist yields the right behavior.
   75: _STOPWORDS: frozenset[str] = frozenset({
   76:     "a", "an", "the", "and", "or", "but", "if", "of", "in", "on", "at",
   77:     "to", "for", "with", "as", "by", "from", "into", "through", "during",
   78:     "before", "after", "above", "below", "between", "is", "are", "was",
   79:     "were", "be", "been", "being", "have", "has", "had", "having", "do",
   80:     "does", "did", "doing", "will", "would", "should", "could", "may",
   81:     "might", "must", "can", "this", "that", "these", "those", "it",
   82:     "its", "their", "there", "they", "them", "we", "us", "our", "you",
   83:     "your", "i", "me", "my", "he", "she", "his", "her",
   84: })
   85: 
   86: _DECIMAL_RE = re.compile(r"\d+(?:\.\d+)?")
   87: _WORD_RE = re.compile(r"[A-Za-z][A-Za-z0-9_\-]+")
   88: 
   89: 
   90: def _min_overlap_threshold() -> int:
   91:     raw = os.environ.get("PG_PROVENANCE_MIN_CONTENT_OVERLAP", "")
   92:     try:
   93:         return max(0, int(raw))
   94:     except ValueError:
   95:         return DEFAULT_MIN_CONTENT_OVERLAP
   96: 
   97: 
   98: def _content_words(text: str) -> set[str]:
   99:     """Lowercase content words (>=3 chars, not stopwords) from `text`."""
  100:     return {
  101:         m.group(0).lower()
  102:         for m in _WORD_RE.finditer(text)
  103:         if len(m.group(0)) >= 3 and m.group(0).lower() not in _STOPWORDS
  104:     }
  105: 
  106: 
  107: def _decimals(text: str) -> set[str]:
  108:     """Numeric tokens (integers + decimals) from `text`."""
  109:     return {m.group(0) for m in _DECIMAL_RE.finditer(text)}
  110:

  164: # Codex APPROVE iter 1. Empirical evidence: I-bug-094 live test ran
  165: # 4/4 audit-derived cases (M2/C2/C1 + positive control) against real
  166: # OpenRouter Gemma 4 31B and got correct verdicts. The cage is
  167: # structurally complete with I-bug-092..097 + I-cj-008; flipping the
  168: # default closes the loop on the 2026-05-09 audit-revealed gap.
  169: #
  170: # Operator escape hatch: `PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT=off` continues
  171: # to disable the check explicitly. Documented per Codex iter-1 brief
  172: # verdict.
  173: _DEFAULT_MODE = "enforce"
  174: 
  175: 
  176: def _entailment_mode() -> str:
  177:     """Return one of 'off', 'warn', 'enforce'. Unknown values map to default.
  178: 
  179:     I-bug-097: emit a single WARNING per process per unrecognized typo
  180:     string. Empty / unset env returns the default mode silently —
  181:     that is the intended default, not a misconfiguration.
  182: 
  183:     I-bug-095: default mode is 'enforce' (was 'off' before this Issue).
  184:     Operators who need the pre-graduation behavior can set
  185:     PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT=off explicitly.
  186:     """
  187:     raw = os.environ.get("PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT", _DEFAULT_MODE).lower().strip()
  188:     if raw and raw not in ("off", "warn", "enforce"):
  189:         if raw not in _UNKNOWN_MODE_WARNED:
  190:             _UNKNOWN_MODE_WARNED.add(raw)
  191:             logger.warning(
  192:                 "PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT=%r unrecognized; "
  193:                 "treating as %r (default). Valid: off, warn, enforce.",
  194:                 raw, _DEFAULT_MODE,
  195:             )
  196:         return _DEFAULT_MODE
  197:     return raw or _DEFAULT_MODE
  198: 
  199: 
  200: # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
  201: # Per-sentence verifier
  202: # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
  203: 
  204: def verify_sentence(
  205:     sentence_text: str,
  206:     pool: EvidencePool,
  207:     min_content_overlap: int | None = None,
  208:     is_synthesis_claim: bool = False,
  209: ) -> tuple[bool, DropReason | None]:
  210:     """Return (verifier_pass, drop_reason) for `sentence_text` against `pool`.
  211: 
  212:     Pass: returns (True, None).
  213:     Fail: returns (False, drop_reason) with reason from DropReason literal.
  214: 
  215:     Implements CLAUDE.md §9.1 invariant 3 in order:
  216:       1. at least one well-formed token        → no_provenance_token
  217:       2. every token references known source_id → invalid_token
  218:       3. spans within source bounds            → span_out_of_range
  219:       4. every decimal in sentence in spans    → numeric_mismatch
  220:       5. >=N shared content words              → overlap_too_low
  221:       6. (I-bug-092) span entails sentence     → entailment_failed
  222:          (gated by PG_STRICT_VERIFY_ENTAILMENT, off by default)
  223: 
  224:     If `is_synthesis_claim=True` AND the sentence has no tokens, return
  225:     (True, None) — synthesis claims pass without provenance by definition
  226:     (I-f5-006). If a synthesis claim DOES carry tokens, that's a generator
  227:     bug; we still run token checks so the underlying invariant holds.
  228:     """
  229:     threshold = (
  230:         min_content_overlap
  231:         if min_content_overlap is not None
  232:         else _min_overlap_threshold()
  233:     )
  234: 
  235:     tokens = extract_tokens(sentence_text)
  236:     if not tokens:
  237:         if is_synthesis_claim:
  238:             return True, None
  239:         return False, "no_provenance_token"
  240: 
  241:     # Validate each token against the pool. Collect span texts for
  242:     # later numeric + overlap checks.
  243:     span_texts: list[str] = []
  244:     for token in tokens:
  245:         reason = validate_token_against_pool(token, pool)
  246:         if reason == "invalid_token":
  247:             return False, "invalid_token"
  248:         if reason == "span_out_of_range":
  249:             return False, "span_out_of_range"
  250:         # Token valid — fetch its span text
  251:         span = get_span_text(token, pool)
  252:         if span is None:
  253:             # Belt-and-suspenders: token validated but get_span_text said no.
  254:             return False, "span_out_of_range"
  255:         span_texts.append(span)
  256: 
  257:     sentence_clean = strip_tokens(sentence_text)
  258:     combined_span = " ".join(span_texts)
  259: 
  260:     # Decimal match: every decimal in sentence must appear in the combined
  261:     # span text. Permits the spans to contain MORE decimals than the
  262:     # sentence cites — that's fine; the constraint is one-way.
  263:     sentence_decimals = _decimals(sentence_clean)
  264:     span_decimals = _decimals(combined_span)
  265:     if not sentence_decimals.issubset(span_decimals):
  266:         return False, "numeric_mismatch"
  267: 
  268:     # Content-word overlap
  269:     sentence_words = _content_words(sentence_clean)
  270:     span_words = _content_words(combined_span)
  271:     # BUG-03 (FX-02, #1106): a truly contentless sentence — NO content words AND no decimals —
  272:     # is dropped EXPLICITLY here (mirrors the provenance_generator floor). With the default
  273:     # threshold=2 it would already fail as overlap_too_low, but this fail-closes the
  274:     # PG_PROVENANCE_MIN_CONTENT_OVERLAP=0 edge (where overlap=0 is not < 0) and gives a precise
  275:     # reason. Faithfulness-TIGHTENING: any content word OR decimal routes to the numeric/overlap
  276:     # floors, so a real clinical sentence can never trip this.
  277:     if not sentence_words and not sentence_decimals:
  278:         return False, "empty_or_contentless_sentence"
  279:     overlap = len(sentence_words & span_words)
  280:     if overlap < threshold:
  281:         return False, "overlap_too_low"
  282: 
  283:     # Check (f) — entailment judge (I-bug-092). Synthesis claims with no
  284:     # tokens already short-circuited above; if a synthesis claim DOES
  285:     # carry tokens it must clear the same content-correctness bar as
  286:     # any other cited sentence.
  287:     mode = _entailment_mode()
  288:     if mode in ("warn", "enforce"):
  289:         verdict, reason = _get_judge().judge(sentence_clean, combined_span)
  290:         _record_judge_outcome(verdict, reason)
  291:         # I-ready-002 (#1071) P0: the judge FAILS OPEN to ("ENTAILED","judge_error:...") on an API/parse
  292:         # error. Counting that as a genuine ENTAILED ships an UNVERIFIED clinical claim as "verified" — a
  293:         # silent downgrade of the binding faithfulness gate (LAW II / lethal-in-clinical). Detect it and
  294:         # FAIL CLOSED in enforce mode (drop the sentence, like an unsupported one); warn-mode logs only.
  295:         if isinstance(reason, str) and reason.startswith("judge_error:"):
  296:             logger.warning(
  297:                 "entailment judge_error (mode=%s): sentence=%r reason=%r — failing closed",
  298:                 mode, sentence_clean, reason,
  299:             )
  300:             if mode == "enforce":
  301:                 return False, "entailment_judge_error_fail_closed"
  302:         if verdict in ("NEUTRAL", "CONTRADICTED"):
  303:             logger.warning(
  304:                 "entailment %s (mode=%s): sentence=%r reason=%r",
  305:                 verdict, mode, sentence_clean, reason,
  306:             )
  307:             if mode == "enforce":
  308:                 return False, "entailment_failed"
  309: 
  310:     return True, None
  311: 
  312: 
  313: def verify_sentence_to_record(
  314:     sentence_text: str,
  315:     section_id: str,
  316:     pool: EvidencePool,
  317:     min_content_overlap: int | None = None,
  318:     is_synthesis_claim: bool = False,
  319: ) -> VerifiedSentence:
  320:     """Convenience: wrap verify_sentence into a VerifiedSentence record.
  321: 
  322:     Used by the generator orchestrator to build Section.verified_sentences.
  323:     """
  324:     passed, reason = verify_sentence(
  325:         sentence_text,
  326:         pool,
  327:         min_content_overlap=min_content_overlap,
  328:         is_synthesis_claim=is_synthesis_claim,
  329:     )
  330:     tokens = [t.raw for t in extract_tokens(sentence_text)]

 3317:     remapping section-local citation numbers to global numbers."""
 3318:     # Each section's biblio has its own 1-based numbering. We need to
 3319:     # renumber globally, but the section's verified_text already has
 3320:     # [1][2][3] markers in section-local space.
 3321:     # Simpler approach: return the raw per-section biblios flattened,
 3322:     # deduped by evidence_id, and let the caller remap the inline
 3323:     # markers in a separate pass.
 3324:     seen: dict[str, dict[str, Any]] = {}
 3325:     for sl in section_slices:
 3326:         for entry in sl:
 3327:             ev_id = entry.get("evidence_id", "")
 3328:             if ev_id and ev_id not in seen:
 3329:                 seen[ev_id] = dict(entry)
 3330:     # Renumber globally
 3331:     final: list[dict[str, Any]] = []
 3332:     for i, entry in enumerate(seen.values(), 1):
 3333:         new_entry = dict(entry)
 3334:         new_entry["num"] = i
 3335:         final.append(new_entry)
 3336:     return final
 3337: 
 3338: 
 3339: def _remap_section_markers_to_global(
 3340:     section_results: list[SectionResult],
 3341:     global_biblio: list[dict[str, Any]],
 3342: ) -> list[str]:
 3343:     """Rewrite each section's [N] markers from section-local to global.
 3344: 
 3345:     Returns a list of remapped section prose strings.
 3346:     """
 3347:     ev_to_global = {b["evidence_id"]: b["num"] for b in global_biblio}
 3348:     remapped: list[str] = []
 3349:     for sect in section_results:
 3350:         if not sect.verified_text:
 3351:             continue
 3352:         # Build a mapping section-local-num -> global-num
 3353:         local_to_global: dict[int, int] = {}
 3354:         for entry in sect.biblio_slice:
 3355:             local_num = entry.get("num")
 3356:             ev_id = entry.get("evidence_id", "")
 3357:             global_num = ev_to_global.get(ev_id)
 3358:             if local_num is not None and global_num is not None:
 3359:                 local_to_global[local_num] = global_num
 3360:         text = sect.verified_text
 3361: 
 3362:         # Replace [N] markers using the mapping. Do the replace with a
 3363:         # callable to avoid subsequent substitutions clobbering each
 3364:         # other (e.g., [1] -> [5] -> [15]).
 3365:         def _replace(match: re.Match) -> str:
 3366:             n = int(match.group(1))
 3367:             g = local_to_global.get(n)
 3368:             return f"[{g}]" if g else match.group(0)
 3369: 
 3370:         text = re.sub(r"\[(\d+)\]", _replace, text)
 3560:         direct_quote_chars = (
 3561:             len(ref_row.get("direct_quote", ""))
 3562:             if ref_row else 0
 3563:         )
 3564:         direct_quote_adequate = direct_quote_chars >= 100
 3565: 
 3566:         # Cited in verified prose? Check bibliography-num citations in
 3567:         # each section's verified_text. Uses ev_id → biblio_num map
 3568:         # that rendered the report.
 3569:         citation_count = 0
 3570:         if selected_ev_id and selected_ev_id in ev_id_to_biblio_num:

  300: def _build_claim_disclosure_doc(multi: Any, quantified_telemetry: Any) -> dict | None:
  301:     """I-cred-008b (#1162): serialize the per-claim CREDIBILITY DISCLOSURE for claim_disclosure.json.
  302: 
  303:     Returns ``None`` (=> no artifact, byte-identical) when ``multi.credibility_analysis`` is absent
  304:     (master flag off). Otherwise returns ``{"sections": [{"title", "claims": [...]}, ...]}`` where each
  305:     claim row carries the six advisory disclosure fields. Sites 1-3 surface via
  306:     ``SectionResult.kept_sentences_pre_resolve``; the quantified path (site 4) has no SectionResult, so
  307:     its rows arrive in ``quantified_telemetry["claim_disclosure"]``. Pure (no IO) for testability.
  308:     """
  309:     if getattr(multi, "credibility_analysis", None) is None:
  310:         return None
  311:     sections: list[dict] = []
  312:     for sr in getattr(multi, "sections", None) or []:
  313:         if getattr(sr, "dropped_due_to_failure", False):
  314:             continue
  315:         kept = getattr(sr, "kept_sentences_pre_resolve", None) or []
  316:         if not kept:
  317:             continue
  318:         sections.append({
  319:             "title": getattr(sr, "title", ""),
  320:             "claims": [
  321:                 {
  322:                     "sentence": getattr(sv, "sentence", ""),
  323:                     "span_verdict": getattr(sv, "span_verdict", ""),
  324:                     "credibility_weight": getattr(sv, "credibility_weight", None),
  325:                     "independent_origin_count": getattr(sv, "independent_origin_count", None),
 1060:                     dst.write(line + "\n")
 1061:                     n += 1
 1062:     except OSError as exc:
 1063:         logging.getLogger(__name__).warning(
 1064:             "per-run cost ledger write failed: %s", exc,
 1065:         )
 1066:     return n

 1069: def filter_verified_sections(sections) -> list:

outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:3:The human gastrointestinal tract harbors an extraordinarily complex, dense, and dynamic microbial ecosystem that functions analogous to a highly specialized endocrine and metabolic organ. This intricate consortium of bacteria, archaea, fungi, and viruses exerts profound influence over systemic host physiology, mucosal immunology, neurological signaling pathways, and overarching metabolic homeostasis. The delicate equilibrium between the host organism and its resident microbiome is continuously mediated by the presence and relative abundance of beneficial probiotic taxa, the exogenous availability of specific fermentable substrates known as prebiotics, and the potentially deleterious effects exerted by opportunistic pathogenic bacteria and their highly toxic metabolic end-products. Achieving a deep, molecular, and ecological understanding of these interconnected components is not merely an academic exercise; it is essential for the optimization of human health, the prevention of chronic inflammatory states, and the mitigation of numerous pathological conditions ranging from metabolic syndrome to cardiorenal diseases. This comprehensive analytical report evaluates the predominant phylogenies of probiotic species, deconstructs the precise mechanistic paradigms defining prebiotics, scrutinizes the toxicological implications of proteolytic fermentation and pathogenic overgrowth, and translates these foundational findings into evidence-based, actionable dietary interventions.
outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:7:The conceptual and practical application of probiotics has historically been dominated by a select group of Gram-positive, lactic acid-producing bacteria. However, recent advancements in high-throughput genomic sequencing, transcriptomics, and anaerobic cultivation technologies have vastly expanded the taxonomic and functional landscape of gut-associated mutualists. The microbial taxa conferring documented health benefits are now broadly and critically categorized into traditional foundational probiotics and highly specialized next-generation probiotics (NGPs), with each category possessing distinct genomic architectures, metabolic pathways, and ecological signatures within the mammalian host.
outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:11:For over a century, the genera *Lactobacillus* and *Bifidobacterium* have served as the absolute foundational pillars of probiotic research, commercial dietary supplementation, and functional food development. Both of these genera are composed of Gram-positive organisms—characterized by the presence of a thick cellular wall composed primarily of peptidoglycan—and both are non-spore-forming entities that produce organic acids as their primary metabolic end-products.1 Despite these superficial similarities, they occupy markedly different ecological niches, possess divergent evolutionary trajectories, and deploy distinct metabolic pathways.
outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:26:Functionally, lactobacilli are exceptional at forming persistent biofilms within both the complex gut and the delicate vaginal microbiota, allowing them to endure harsh environmental conditions and maintain ample, stable populations.1 Within the vaginal tract, *Lactobacillus* species are normally the major, dominant component of the microbiota, where they exert a mutualistic relationship with the human body.1 They protect the host against potential invasions by urogenital and enteric pathogens through competitive exclusion, the lowering of luminal pH via lactic acid production, and the secretion of specific bacteriocins.1 When deployed therapeutically, they are among the most common and effective probiotics utilized in clinical settings to treat infectious diarrhea, bacterial vaginosis, and dermatological immune disorders such as atopic eczema, though consumers are strictly advised to select products containing demonstrably live bacteria or highly preserved freeze-dried formulations to ensure cellular viability and therapeutic efficacy.1 Furthermore, clinical studies have demonstrated significant therapeutic benefits when combining distinct strains of *Bifidobacterium* and *Lactobacillus* for the targeted treatment of complex inflammatory bowel diseases (IBD).2
outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:30:While the traditional foundational probiotics successfully promote general digestive homeostasis and basic barrier protection, contemporary advancements in high-resolution microbiome profiling, synthetic biology, and omics technologies have catalyzed the discovery and clinical characterization of Next-Generation Probiotics (NGPs).11 These organisms represent a paradigm shift in biotherapeutic application. These strictly anaerobic, historically difficult-to-cultivate bacterial species—predominantly featuring *Akkermansia muciniphila*, *Faecalibacterium prausnitzii*, and specific *Eubacterium* and *Bacteroides* species—exhibit highly targeted, complex mechanistic pathways.11 Unlike conventional, food-derived probiotics, NGPs are meticulously selected based on their highly specific functional roles within the host biology, linking them directly to the targeted regulation of metabolic syndrome, chronic inflammatory conditions, immune dysregulation, and even the modulation of neurological and cognitive states via the gut-brain axis.11 Because of their specific, potent actions, the development of NGPs is increasingly aimed toward rigorous pharmaceutical applications and regulatory pathways, earning them the designation of live biotherapeutic products (LBPs).14
outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:51:To successfully sustain, nourish, and specifically manipulate the delicate populations of both traditional foundational probiotics and advanced next-generation probiotics within the highly competitive gut environment, the continuous provision of specific, highly targeted dietary substrates is an absolute biological requirement. While it is universally true that a vast majority of complex dietary fibers are eventually fermented by various microbes in the gut, the simple act of general fermentation alone is entirely insufficient to scientifically classify a given dietary ingredient as a true prebiotic.17
outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:61:3. **Mandatory Microbiome-Mediated Mechanism of Action**: A crucial distinction must be drawn between the definitions of probiotics and prebiotics. Unlike the definition of probiotics—which does not explicitly require researchers to define the precise mechanism of action leading to the host health benefit—the modern prebiotic definition strictly stipulates one.18 A prebiotic-induced health benefit *must* undeniably derive from the direct modulation of the host microbiome (either its composition or its exact function) as a direct biological result of the selective utilization of the substrate.18 A sound, deeply biological rationale must be developed explaining precisely how the observed pattern of selective microbial utilization mechanistically leads to the physiological health benefit.18  
outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:65:Importantly, while substrates such as pharmaceutical antibiotics, isolated dietary minerals, vitamins, and lytic bacteriophages might drastically alter the composition of the host microbiota, they do not meet the strict mechanistic criteria and are categorically excluded from being defined as prebiotics.19 Furthermore, the field actively distinguishes prebiotics from the emerging concept of postbiotics. An ISAPP panel later defined a postbiotic strictly as a "preparation of inanimate microorganisms and/or their components that confers a health benefit on the host," which must contain inactivated microbial cells or cell components, alongside their metabolites.4 These inanimate postbiotics exert their effects via the delivery of intact biomolecules—such as direct antimicrobial lactic acid, targeted bacteriocins, quorum sensing and quenching molecules, and protective adhesins like fimbriae and lectins that survive processing—or through secreted proteins like Msp1/p75, Msp1/p40, and HM0539 originating from model probiotics like *Lactobacillus rhamnosus* GG, which enhance epithelial barrier function entirely independently of live cellular colonization.4
outputs/audits/beatboth5\gemini_sanitized\Q76_gut_microbiota.md:104:Paradoxically, and highly critically for nutritional science, the careless dietary ingestion of certain common commercial probiotics that contain highly potent D-lactic acid-producing strains (such as specific *Lactobacillus* species frequently found in highly cultured milk drinks or heavily concentrated capsule supplements) can directly provoke, trigger, or massively exacerbate severe episodes of D-lactic acidosis in these highly vulnerable SBS patients.31 For instance, highly popular commercial cultured milk drinks (often designated in studies as brands Y and F) have been shown to contain massive, high contents of dangerous D-lactate, particularly when stored at room temperature, reaching levels of up to 0.52 g/L.31 While these specific D-lactic acid-producing probiotics and their associated fermented products pose virtually no clinically relevant adverse health effects in healthy, physiologically normal individuals, they pose a severe, life-threatening risk to those with altered gastrointestinal anatomy.31 This stark biological reality forcefully underscores the absolute, critical medical need for deep, strain-specific evaluation and immense caution when blindly administering live biotherapeutics to complex patient populations.

outputs/audits/beatboth5\drb_76_polaris.md:44:Although serious adverse effects have been rare in probiotic trials, they have not been fully reported, and one trial in severe acute pancreatitis found higher mortality in the probiotic arm.[4] In a retrospective registry study across five Finnish university hospitals from 2009 to 2018, 46 patients had Saccharomyces fungemia; at least 20 (43%) were using S. boulardii probiotic, and compared with a control group of patients with bacteremia or candidemia, the odds ratio for probiotic use was 14 (95% CI 4–44).[4] The study also described a case where probiotic administration through a displaced percutaneous endoscopic gastrostomy tube led to peritoneal contamination and fatal septic shock.[4] The authors conclude that S. cerevisiae var. boulardii probiotics are not recommended for patients with indwelling catheters, who are immunocompromised, or who are critically ill.[4] A prior meta-analysis had identified 37 cases of Saccharomyces fungemia linked to probiotic use, and an additional 14 reports with 22 cases were published after that review, bringing the total to 59 cases before this study.[4] The gut microbiota normally provides colonization resistance against pathogens, contributing to host defense.[5] Therefore, current evidence advises against routine probiotic use in patients with central venous catheters, immunosuppression, or critical illness.[4] The Finnish study indicates that probiotic use should be considered carefully for patients with compromised gastrointestinal integrity.[4] A meta-analysis of five studies found a pooled risk ratio of 0.63 (95% CI 0.29–1.37) for probiotics in preventing Clostridioides difficile infection in adults, with nonsignificant results.[4]

According to the International Society of Sports Nutrition (ISSN), probiotic supplementation can optimize the health, performance, and recovery of athletes at all stages of their careers. Recent research suggests that probiotics can improve immune system functions, reduce gastrointestinal distress, and increase gut permeability in athletes. Additionally, probiotics may provide athletes with secondary health benefits that could positively affect athletic performance through enhanced recovery from fatigue, improved immune function, and maintenance of healthy gastrointestinal tract function. The integration of some probiotic strains into athletes' diets and the consumption of multi‐strain compounds may lead to an improvement in performance and can positively affect performance‐related aspects such as fatigue, muscle pain, body composition, and cardiorespiratory fitness. In summary, probiotics can be beneficial for athletes at all stages of their careers, from amateur to professional.    42: ### Safety
   43: 
   44: Although serious adverse effects have been rare in probiotic trials, they have not been fully reported, and one trial in severe acute pancreatitis found higher mortality in the probiotic arm.[4] In a retrospective registry study across five Finnish university hospitals from 2009 to 2018, 46 patients had Saccharomyces fungemia; at least 20 (43%) were using S. boulardii probiotic, and compared with a control group of patients with bacteremia or candidemia, the odds ratio for probiotic use was 14 (95% CI 4–44).[4] The study also described a case where probiotic administration through a displaced percutaneous endoscopic gastrostomy tube led to peritoneal contamination and fatal septic shock.[4] The authors conclude that S. cerevisiae var. boulardii probiotics are not recommended for patients with indwelling catheters, who are immunocompromised, or who are critically ill.[4] A prior meta-analysis had identified 37 cases of Saccharomyces fungemia linked to probiotic use, and an additional 14 reports with 22 cases were published after that review, bringing the total to 59 cases before this study.[4] The gut microbiota normally provides colonization resistance against pathogens, contributing to host defense.[5] Therefore, current evidence advises against routine probiotic use in patients with central venous catheters, immunosuppression, or critical illness.[4] The Finnish study indicates that probiotic use should be considered carefully for patients with compromised gastrointestinal integrity.[4] A meta-analysis of five studies found a pooled risk ratio of 0.63 (95% CI 0.29–1.37) for probiotics in preventing Clostridioides difficile infection in adults, with nonsignificant results.[4]

codex
P1 findings:

1. F02 is not fixed in production. The new regression replaces the entailment judge with a fake `NEUTRAL` verdict, then asserts the existing gate drops `NEUTRAL`: [test file](C:/POLARIS/tests/dr_benchmark/test_lane_b_gen_faithfulness_completeness_beatboth5.py:113). The same file explicitly says the real Gemma judge returned `ENTAILED` for the boulardii-to-probiotics widening and that prompt work is “reported, not fixed”: [lines 13-18](C:/POLARIS/tests/dr_benchmark/test_lane_b_gen_faithfulness_completeness_beatboth5.py:13). The live gate just trusts the judge verdict and only drops `NEUTRAL`/`CONTRADICTED`: [provenance_generator.py](C:/POLARIS/src/polaris_graph/generator/provenance_generator.py:1478). Since the real defect is “judge says ENTAILED for a scope-widened claim,” this is a continuing P1/no-op for #1176.

2. C07 introduces a zero-verified abort regression. Gap stubs are emitted with `sentences_verified=0` but `dropped_due_to_failure=False` and non-empty `verified_text`: [multi_section_generator.py](C:/POLARIS/src/polaris_graph/generator/multi_section_generator.py:2149), [zero-kept path](C:/POLARIS/src/polaris_graph/generator/multi_section_generator.py:2342). The runner’s zero-verified abort still uses `filter_verified_sections()`, which treats “not dropped + non-empty verified_text” as survived verification: [run_honest_sweep_r3.py](C:/POLARIS/scripts/run_honest_sweep_r3.py:1069), and that predicate gates the abort path: [run_honest_sweep_r3.py](C:/POLARIS/scripts/run_honest_sweep_r3.py:5853). I verified a gap stub returns as “verified” from that filter. The new C07 test hand-copies the render predicate instead of calling the live abort predicate: [test file](C:/POLARIS/tests/dr_benchmark/test_lane_b_gen_faithfulness_completeness_beatboth5.py:277).

C06: no blocking issue found. The benchmark constant covers the scope-template inventory plus original clinical defaults, `setdefault` preserves overrides, and the env is set before the lazy `frame_fetcher` import path. The reimport test is useful for the import-time freeze, though not a true fetch-level end-to-end test.

Verification run: `python -m pytest tests/dr_benchmark/test_lane_b_gen_faithfulness_completeness_beatboth5.py -q` passed, with only a pytest cache permission warning.
